Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The aim of this project was to analyse the gene synteny (the genetic context of genes) and
gene flux in bacteria, specifically in E.coli and Shigella spp., to understand the mechanisms
of gene mobility. This thesis presented a stand-alone Snakemake pipeline, that can be used
to analyse the gene flux in bacteria. It does so by calculating a selection of metrics - k-mer
distance, flank identity index, and ANI - that best describe the differences between the
flanking sequences. It then employs Procrustes analysis and Mantel test to calculate the
correlation between the flanking regions and the whole genome phylogeny. The pipeline’s
capabilities were demonstrated by analysing 3503 E.coli and Shigella spp. genomes, along
with a selected set of 6343 core and accessory genes. This work has demonstrated the
potential of the pipeline to analyse the gene flux in any bacterial species.
ii
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli

Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:6==
1
Introduction
1.1
E.coli and Shigella genetic background
Escherichia coli and Shigella species are closely related Gram-negative, facultative anaer-
obic bacteria belonging to the family Enterobacteriaceae. The four Shigella species (S.
dysenteriae, S. flexneri, S. boydii and S. sonnei) are believed to be pathovars that be-
long to the E. coli species, sharing close evolutionary history [1]. These bacteria cause a
highly contagious intestinal infection, called Shigellosis [2]. S. sonnei and S. flexneri are
the most researched Shigella species, as they are responsible for more than 95% cases of
Shigellosis worldwide [3]. The classification of the bacteria to the Shigella group has been
preserved mostly due to its historical significance and use in the medical setting [1]. The
E.coli and Shigella species show 80-90% of nucleotide identity and share a core genome
of approximately 3 Mb, with the number of core genes ranging from approximately 2000
to 3000 [4, 5, 6, 7]. The pathogenicity of Shigella spp. depends on the presence of a large
(210-230 kb) virulence plasmid pINV, that encodes approximately 100 genes facilitating
the invasion of host cells. The evolutionary event that has led to the emergence of distinct
Shigella species was the acquisition of this plasmid [3].
The E. coli strains can be divided into eight phylogenetic groups labeled A, B1, B2, C,
D, E, F and G, when classified using accepted PCR Clermont method [8]. E.coli strains
that do not fall within these groups can be categorised to two cryptic clades: cladeI and
cladeV [8]. It is recognized that E.coli phylogroups have been found to have different
representation in various environments and hosts.
Additionally, they vary in terms of
their morphological and genotypic traits, as well as in their pathological potential [9].
Recent mash-based approaches of classifying over 10 000 E.coli and Shigella genomes, have
suggested that the phylogeny of this species may be more complex. Based on variations
in phylogenetic profiles, variations in conserved genes within and between phylogroups as
well as different gain and loss rates of protein families, the existence of 14 distinct E.coli
phylogroups was proposed [10]. Additionally, the study revealed that two of these groups
(Shig1 and Shig2) were populated exclusively by Shigella genomes, pointing to a distinct
evolutionary origin. Furthermore, over 83% of all the sequenced genomes were placed in
the two groups, sharing a unique set of core genes and lower G-C content. The remainder
of genomes were distributed amongst seven additional phylogroups, with the majority
belonging to the B1 group, indicating that this group is most likely where the ancestors
of these Shigella strains, especially those in Shig1, originated. This has further confirmed
the currently accepted theory, that the four Shigella species have emerged from multiple
evolutionary paths [11, 10].
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
1
==PAGE:7==
1.2
Gene flux
In order to survive, bacteria have developed evolutionary mechanisms to adapt to changing
environmental conditions. One of these mechanisms is gene flux, the process of gain, loss,
and transfer of genes. In bacteria, such as E. coli and Shigella, gene flux is influenced by
a process of gene exchange even between distant species, called horizontal gene transfer
(HGT). Transposons, plasmids, phages, and other mobile elements play a major role in
enabling HGT [12].
HGT in bacteria is facilitated by one of three mechanisms. The DNA can be acquired
directly from the environment in the process of transformation. Through conjugation,
two distinct bacterial cells can transfer genetic material between each other through a
pilus. And lastly, the genes can be carried and transferred to bacteria by bacteriophages
in the process of transduction [13]. The genes, that are subject to HGT usually belong
to the accessory genome of bacteria [12].
These genes are not shared among all the
strains within the species and are often not essential for the organism’s survival. However,
accessory genes can give a strain the ability to survive under specific conditions, giving the
organism a selective advantage (antibiotic resistance, pathogenicity etc.) [14]. One of the
prime examples of how HGT can shape the evolution, is the case of Shiga-Toxin Producing
E.coli. These have acquired the ability to produce Shiga toxin, the key element in their
virulence, causing infection outbreaks. These genes are often located on bacteriophages
that have integrated into the bacterial genome [15]. Generally, E.coli are known to retain
a very large set of accessory genes. Although accessory genes are often believed to not be
essential for the survival of the species, it has been shown that in some cases, a combination
of multiple accessory genes can give an organism a selective advantage. This results in their
retention, even though alone they might be subject to deletions. Patterns of co-occurrence
of genes, that vary by lineage, can be identified, but are still subject to research [16].
In contrast to accessory genes, the core genome which is shared among all the strains
within the species, is evolved and inherited vertically. The evolution via vertical gene
transfer (VGT) is driven mainly by the introduction of random mutations in the DNA,
that are favoured in the selection process. Due to its predictability, VGT is the primary
focus of understanding bacterial evolution [17]. HGT in particular, has proven problematic
in inferring phylogeny when unrelated lineages, that acquired foreign genetic material from
the same source, can mistakenly appear to be related [18]. There are phylogenetic methods
to detect the presence of HGT, when the whole species phylogeny tree and the single gene
phylogeny tree are compared. Genes that are inherited vertically are assumed to evolve at
the same rates as the whole species. As a result, vertically evolving genes will share the
phylogeny of the entire species. When HGT is introduced, it causes differences between
species and gene distances, resulting in large differences between gene and whole genome
phylogenies. Parametric methods include the analysis of factors, such as G-C content,
dinucleotide frequencies or codon usage of the whole genome compared to the one of
a single gene. When the G-C content of a gene is significantly different, compared to
2
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:8==
the one of the entire species, it is likely, that HGT took place [19]. However, numerous
factors influence the G-C content and codon usage pattern, therefore differences in G-C
content should serve only as an indicator of HGT, and should be supported by other
methods [20]. The introduction of a new gene by HGT can be also detected by analysing
the gene synteny, genetic context of genes. Flanking regions containing sequence regions
of transposable elements, mobilisation sequences related to phages or insertion sites can
indicate that a gene insertion took place [18]. Techniques of using gene synteny to detect
HGT and infer phylogeny have been described [21, 22, 23]. All mentioned methods build
upon the ”Synteny Index”, describing the average synteny between a pair of genomes
as the number of rearrangements required to turn one genome into the other. Synteny-
based approach allows for better detection of HGT, when occurring between genomes with
similar nucleotide composition and codon usage, as well as between more closely related
genomes, where phylogeny and parameter methods may fall short [18].
1.3
Flankophile
HGT also plays a major role in the spread of antimicrobial resistance (AMR). Antimi-
crobial resistance genes (ARGs) are transferred between bacteria via plasmids or trans-
posons. This allows for rapid spread of AMR, even beyond the context of single species
[24]. Thanks to HGT, ARGs are often found at different locations within the genome and
their surrounding sequences can give insight into their evolutionary origin. ARGs served
as the target genes when demonstrating the capabilities of Flankophile, a Snakemake [25]
pipeline, capable of analysis and visualisation of gene synteny, the genetic context of
genes [26]. The upstream and downstream flanking regions (flanks) are analysed in this
pipeline. Using Flankophile, a user is able to analyse synteny of their own target gene
reference database in a large number of genome assemblies. The genomes are searched for
matches to the reference database using ABRicate [27]. Sequence variants are identified
in this step. Consequently, the identified hits are then checked for flanking region length
and the ones with insuﬀicient flank lengths are filtered out. The sequences that meet the
criteria are then extracted from the whole genome sequence and CDhit [28] is used to
cluster the sequences based on their identity. The flanking regions of each gene cluster
are annotated using Prokka and paired with the variant results from ABRicate to plot
gene synteny. The output of the pipeline are synteny plots, containing the distance trees
of the sequences, sequence variant of the hit, that corresponds to each tree tip and the
visual representation of flank sequence annotations (Figure 1.1)[26].
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
3
==PAGE:9==
Figure 1.1: Visual guide to gene synteny plots produced by Flankophile [26]
1.4
Research question
The research of gene flux in bacteria, such as E.coli and Shigella spp., has revealed that
such organisms are capable of rapid evolutionary change and adaptation of their genetic
material, through exchanging and acquiring new genes. The analyses of genetic context of
genes - gene synteny - of both the core and accessory genes could provide valuable infor-
mation on the gene mobility in bacteria. Studies have so far focused on genes, known to
exhibit high mobility, such as ARGs [26]. This project aims to shift the focus to a broader
analysis of all genes, both core and accessory.
The broader approach will allow us to
gain an understanding of the gene flux, including highly mobile genes from the accessory
genome, and also the ones that are considered to be stably positioned within the genome
sequence across many genetic lineages. We aim to use Flankophile as the main analysis
tool, to obtain the k-mer distance matrices of each gene’s flanking regions. Distances be-
tween whole-genomes, target genes and their flanking environments will then be analyzed
together in this project. The comparison of the flanking regions and whole genomes will
be conducted using Procrustes analysis [29] and Mantel test [30], statistical methods fre-
quently used for distance matrix comparison. A gene that has steadily evolved and been
inherited vertically will exhibit flank distances that closely resemble whole-genome dis-
tances. In contrast, the absence of correlation with the whole strain phylogeny will imply
HGT. In this context, the focus of this research is to develop a method for quantifying
the ability of specific genes to move within and between genomes. The pipeline will be
developed and tested on complete E.coli and Shigella reference genomes. However, this
work will result in a universal pipeline, that could be tested to quantify the gene-specific
mobility of any bacterial species in order to gain deeper understanding of its evolution.
The results will be analysed in terms of gene function, and paired with corresponding
KEGG [31] and GO term [32] annotations.

Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:10==
2
Materials and Methods
2.1
Pipeline overview
In this section, a general overview of the pipeline, designed to analyse the gene flux of core
and accessory genes in bacteria is presented (Figure 2.1). The pipeline was developed using
Snakemake (v 6.9.1) [25], a tool to implement bioinformatic workflows. In order to execute
the pipeline, Miniconda (v 4.11.0) [33] has to be installed. The Conda environment will
then be set-up automatically during the pipeline’s initial run. This pipeline was designed
and tested on Computerome2 (The Danish National Supercomputer for Life Sciences), as
it requires large computational resources. The number of CPU cores is 40 by default and
is automatically scaled down when less resources are available. The pipeline is available
to download from GitHub at: https://github.com/MartinaVojtkova/GeneFluxPipeline.

Figure 2.1: Pipeline Overview
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
5
==PAGE:11==
This pipeline’s primary input is a tab-separated file that links each genome sample to
its corresponding FASTA genomic data file. The path to the file and the pipeline’s other
parameters can be specified in the config.yaml file provided in the repository. Additionally,
a user can define the length of the upstream/downstream flanking regions (the default value
is 10 000 base pairs). The input genomes are automatically annotated. Next, pangenome
analysis is performed, which serves as a base for gene selection. Selected sample of core and
accessory genes is then subject to gene synteny analysis. The k-mer Hamming distance,
flank identity index (FI) and average nucleotide identity (ANI) matrices are calculated.
Whole genome distances/similarities are determined using dRep [34]. Distance differences
between whole-genomes, target genes and their flanking environments are then analyzed
using Procrustes analysis. Correlation of ANI of the flanking regions and whole genome
similarity is calculated using Mantel test. The main output of the pipeline is a tabulated
file containing the list of genes, their calculated similarity metrics: FI index median, K-
mer distance median, ANI median, the results of Procrustes analysis - the correlation with
the whole genome distances and the significance value, and the results of Mantel test -
Mantel statistic and the significance value. Complete matrices for individual genes are
also generated as output.
The methods employed in the pipeline will be illustrated on a practical example using the
genomes of E.coli and Shigella spp.
2.2
Input: E.coli and Shigella spp. genome data
Genome data for the large scale E.coli and Shigella spp. genome analysis was obtained
from National Center of Biotechnology Information (NCBI) [35], specifically from the
Reference Sequence Database (RefSeq) [36]. RefSeq was chosen for its curated genome
data and reviewed sequences in order to make sure no contamination is present within
the dataset. The whole E.coli phylogeny found in NCBI [37] was visualised and analysed
using FigTree (v 1.4.4) [38] (Figure 2.2). Next, a sub-set of genomes that were evenly
distributed around the phylogeny tree was selected to achieve balanced representation of
all E.coli strains, and at the same time prevent the over-representation of individual E.
coli strains, as this could potentially introduce bias into the results. Furthermore, the
phylogroups were determined using ClermonTyping [39], an in silico tool for Escherichia
genus strain phylotyping, to ensure even distribution.
Only complete genome and chromosome sequences were included in the analysis. This
allowed to capture a larger context of genes, compared to contig genome data.
The
dataset was obtained using NCBI’s datasets command-line tool (v 15.29.0), which enabled
effective downloading. A total of 3503 genomes were used in the analysis, comprising of
3306 E. coli, 27 S. dysenteriae, 112 S. flexneri, 19 S. boydii and 40 S. sonnei genomes. The
sample names and paths to their FASTA files were then summarized in a tab-separated
file and used as input for the pipeline.

Figure 2.2: Full E. coli taxonomy from NCBI [37]: Genomes selected as pipeline’s input
for large-scale analysis are shown in blue and cover the whole phylogeny tree.
2.3
Whole genome comparison
The whole genome comparison is performed as the first step of the pipeline using dRep, a
program used for rapid pairwise comparison of genome sets [34]. For this purpose, dRep’s
compare function is used. It enables faster comparison of the genomes, by using MASH
algorithm to perform primary clustering, thus eliminating unnecessary pairwise compar-
isons. MASH is sensitive to genome incomplteness, resulting in very low MASH identities
for genomes of low completeness, even when the genomes are identical [40]. Therefore, the
primary clustering threshold should be set lower, when working with incomplete genome
assemblies. The secondary comparison is performed using dRep’s default ANImf com-
parison algorithm. ANImf, based on ANIn algorithm [41], first aligns the whole genome
sequences using nucmer and then introduces an additional step of filtering these align-
ments, so that each region of genome 1 is only allowed to align with one sequence region
in genome 2. This approach increases the accuracy for genomes that contain repetitive se-
quence regions [40]. The thresholds for primary and secondary clustering can be specified
as the pipeline’s parameters in the config.yaml file. The genome similarities/distances are
reported in the Mbd.csv output file. These are then converted to pairwise genome distance
and similarity matrix, using reshape2 (v 1.4.4) [42] R package. Both matrices will be used
in later steps of the pipeline.
In this project, only complete genomes from RefSeq are used, allowing to increase the
primary cluster threshold and thus decrease the computational time. The primary cluster
threshold was set to 99% in order to cluster only genomes of high similarity, thus reducing
the number of pairwise ANI comparisons, which are computationally demanding. The
secondary cluster threshold for ANImf pair-wise comparison kept at a default dRep value
(0.95).
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
7
==PAGE:13==
2.4
Genome annotation
Next, the genomes are annotated using Prokka (v 1.14.6) [43], a software tool for rapid
bacterial genome annotation.
The pipeline does not use any pre-existing annotations,
and instead favors performing its own annotating in order to ensure consistency across the
samples. Prokka was selected because of its ability to generate comprehensive and accurate
annotations, which are necessary for detailed genomic analysis.
Prokka first identifies
candidate genes and their sequence coordinates using Prodigal. Next, BLAST is used to
first annotate the core set of genes. Following this step, three additional databases are
searched using HMMER3: ISfinder, NCBI Bacterial Antimicrobial Resistance Reference
Gene Database and UniProtKB (SwissProt). These IDs are later used to calculate the
flank identity index (Section 2.9.2). If a candidate gene does not flag a match in any of
these databases, it is labeled as ”hypothetical protein”.
This pipeline is meant for the analysis of only bacterial species, therefore the ”Bacteria”
kingdom is pre-defined for annotation and cannot be changed. The default Prokka settings
are used, including a minimum contig size of ”1,” and an E-value cut-off of ”1e-06,” among
others. The genus parameter can be specified in the config.yaml file. In this project, the
genus parameter was set to Escherichia.
2.5
Pangenome analysis
The master annotations in GFF3 format, containing both sequences and annotations, are
then used to perform pangenome analysis using Roary (v 3.1.13) [44]. Pangenome is a set
of all genes from all strains within a species. Roary identifies shared and unique genes in
a set of input genomes, allowing the user to better understand the genetic diversity of the
dataset. It provides information on the composition of core and accessory genomes in the
analysed bacterial species. It does this by extracting and translating gene coding regions,
pre-clustering protein sequences, and then using BLASTp and MCL algorithms to identify
orthologous gene groups [44].
The core gene alignment in Roary is performed using MAFFT [45], specified by the -n
option. The minimum percentage identity for BLASTp is set to 95. The default behavior of
Roary is to detect and split paralogs. By considering gene synteny Roary groups paralogs
into the most appropriate cluster. This would interfere with the pipeline’s analysis of
gene synteny (genes with differing syntenies would be separated into different clusters),
therefore the option to split paralogs is disabled. The parameter -ap is used to allow these
paralogs in the core gene alignment. By default, Roary defines a maximum number of
gene clusters that are allowed to be identified in the data. After this threshold is reached,
the analysis gets terminated and the core gene alignment will not be performed. The
default number of gene clusters in Roary is 50 000. However, in this pipeline, the default
is increased to 70 000 to account for possible larger diversity in the data (for example the
8
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:14==
presence of both Escherichia and Shigella genomes in the data) and to avoid interruptions
in the pipeline.
Roary generates several output files important for downstream analysis. First, the sum-
mary_statistics.txt, stating the number of genes in the core and accessory genome. Sec-
ondly, it outputs gene_presence_absence.csv spreadsheet listing each gene, its occurrence
and the genomes it is present in. Next, a binary matrix with the presence and absence of
each gene, the clustered_proteins file stating the member genes of each gene cluster and
lastly, an alignment of core genes [44], which can be used to infer phylogeny of the input
genomes it downstream analysis of results.
2.6
Gene selection
Based on the results of pangenome analysis, the pipeline automatically generates a list
of genes to be analysed, spanning evenly over the core and accessory genomes. By de-
fault, Roary generates a binary matrix with the presence and absence of each gene in the
genomes. However, this matrix provides information solely on the presence or absence of
genes within genomes. It does not consider the specific count or number of copies of each
gene within individual genomes. To address this, the clustered_proteins file from Roary
is used to calculate a new presence-absence matrix, taking into account not only gene
presence but also the number of copies of each gene within the genome sequences.
Median count (number of copies) of each gene is calculated, considering only the subset
of genomes where the gene was present. Genes that are present as single copies in the
majority of genomes are selected. This is done by filtering those genes, that have their
median count equal to one. This selection process enables to account for genes that may
have undergone duplication or multiplication events in only a subset of genomes, providing
a more comprehensive view of gene variations. Selected genes are then further filtered. The
genes that were present in less than 10 genomes are removed by default. The remainder
of genes is sampled over, in order to reduce the gene set to a portion of its original size,
allowing for a more focused and computationally feasible analysis. The sampling step
can be defined in the pipeline’s configuration file as ”slice_size”. The default value is 1,
meaning the pipeline does not perform additional filtering. For this project, the size was
set to 3, selecting only a third of the genes for further analysis.
The list of genes selected for further analysis is saved into gene_list.txt file.
2.7
Flank sequence extraction
In this step, the prepared list of genes is used to extract sequence coordinates of each gene
from annotation GFF files. The sequence positions serve as a basis to construct .BED
files. These contain the start and end position of each gene’s flanking region. The starting
and ending position of a target gene is extracted from GFF file, and the length of desired
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
9
==PAGE:15==
flank is subtracted/added to these positions. The default length of the flanking regions
is 10 000 base pairs, but can be modified by the user. Sequences of the flanking regions
are extracted from the whole genome sequences using BEDtools [46]. Target genes, that
have surrounding sequences shorter than the desired flank length (10kb) are filtered out
in this step. Each flanking region is saved in its individual FASTA file. These are then
merged based on the gene clusters they belong to. A multiFASTA file containing flanking
region sequences of one gene cluster from all genome samples is obtained as a result.
Second database is generated by masking the target gene sequence, by substituting its
DNA sequence with ”N” string.
2.8
Flank annotation extraction
Similiar to the extraction of DNA sequences, the annotations of the flanking regions are
extracted. The master annotation files from Prokka are indexed using a python script,
creating a dictionary structure. These indexes, together with previously generated BED-
files, are used to retrieve annotation of the individual genes and their flanking regions.
Annotation of each flank is saved to a separate file. These are merged to create flank
annotation databases in GFF3 format for each gene cluster.
2.9
Metrics for assessing flank similarity
In order to assess how similar the flanking regions of a gene are, three metrics are cal-
culated: k-mer distance, average nucleotide identity and flank identity index (measure of
annotation similarity). For each gene a pair-wise distance/similarity matrix is produced,
containing distances between each pair of genomes, where said gene is present. The se-
quence database, where the target gene is masked is used in this step. Next, a median
of every matrix (lower triangle, excluding the diagonal) is calculated as a single per-gene
measure of flanking region similarity/distance. The median was chosen, instead of the
mean, to make the metric more representative of the whole data and to make it more
robust towards outliers.
2.9.1
K-mer distance
The calculation of the k-mer distance is performed using KMA (v 1.4.0) [47]. First, each
of the flank sequence databases is indexed using KMA index. The size of the k-mer for
index building can be specified in the configuration file. The size of the k-mer determines
the sensitivity and specificity of the mapping. In this project, k-mers of size 16 bp are used
index the database, as they are long enough to avoid matching of unrelated sequences and
short enough to not miss relevant sequences. The indexed databases are used to calculate
lower triangular k-mer distance matrices for each gene cluster, using KMA dist.
The
pipeline uses k-mer Hamming distance as the default distance measure. The Hamming
distance is suitable for comparing sequences of the same length, in this case, flanking
10
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:16==
region sequences of one gene cluster. When comparing the distance between two gene
sequences using this metric, the Hamming distance is defined as the number of positions
(bases) at which the corresponding k-mers from the two sequences differ. Additionally,
this method is computationally eﬀicient, making it suitable for analysing large datasets.
The Snakemake rules for KMA execution were adapted from Flankophile [26] and adjusted
to work in this project’s workflow.
2.9.2
Flank identity index
The Flank Identity index (FI) matrix is created for each gene to compare the genomic
annotations between different flanking regions. The FI is a measure used to compare the
annotations (Prokka identified genes) of a pair of flanking regions. The flank annotation
databases (Section 2.8) are used for this purpose. The GFF is parsed and relevant UniPro-
tKB and ISfinder IDs are extracted from the ”attributes” information in each entry. Using
the names of the corresponding sequence regions, extracted IDs are grouped to create a
dictionary structure. For each pair of genomic samples, the number of genes (UniProtKB
and ISfinder IDs) shared between their flanking regions is calculated. The flank identity
percentage for each pair is calculated by dividing by the average number of genes in the
flanking regions. The calculation of the flank index requires two well annotated genes to
be present in both flanking regions. In instances, where the flanking regions are composed
solely of ”hypothetical proteins”, the flank index can not be calculated and such genes are
excluded from the calculation. The lower triangle of the FI matrix, excluding the diagonal,
is used to calculate the median FI scores. These metrics provided an overall assessment
of the genetic similarity between the flanking regions studied.
2.9.3
Average nucleotide identity
Average nucleotide identity (ANI) is calculated as the third metric of flanking region simi-
larity between genomes. FastANI (v 1.34) [48] was used for this purpose. It first identifies
homologous sequences between the flanking regions being compared. FastANI provides
a computationally effective way of ANI calculation, when instead of using alignments to
identify these homologous sequences, it uses a Mash-based algorithm without compromis-
ing accuracy. Then, ANI is defined as the average nucleotide identity of the sequence
pairs shared by two flanking regions. For orthologous sequences originating from the same
species the ANI usually falls within range 95-100% [48]. In all instances where ANI is
below 80%, the ANI will not be calculated, and NA’s are reported. A phylip-formatted
matrix is generated as output.
2.10
Whole genome and flank comparison
As the last step of the pipeline, the relationship between whole genome distances and gene
flanking region distances is analysed using Generalised Procrustes analysis and Mantel
Test. For Procrustes analysis, the k-mer distance matrices of flanking regions (calculated
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
11
==PAGE:17==
from masked gene sequences) and the whole genome distance matrix from dRep are used.
For Mantel test, the ANI matrices of the flanking regions and the whole genome similarity
matrix from dRep are used.
2.10.1
Symmetric Procrustes analysis
Whole genome matrix reduction
The whole genome distance matrix generated by dRep (Section 2.3) contains pair-wise
distances of all input genomes in the dataset. On the other hand, the number of genomes
in each gene’s distance matrix can vary, depending on whether the gene is present or
absent in the input genomes. The full whole genome distance matrix, can therefore be
used only in comparison to the core gene’s flanking region distances. For this reason, the
full genome distance matrix has to be reduced to only contain the same genomes as in the
case of k-mer distance matrix for each individual pair-wise Procrustes analysis.
K-mer distance normalisation
In order to effectively compare the whole genome dissimilarity and flank k-mer distance
matrices, the k-mer matrix needs to be normalised and the range of the data has to be
standardised. The whole genome distance matrix contains values ranging from 0-1, where
zero represents 100% identity, and one represents 100% dissimilarity.
In contrast, the
k-mer distance matrix contains distances that extend into tens of thousands. A minimum
and maximum value of each flanking region distance matrix is found and the matrix is
normalised using min-max normalisation [49] to range from 0-1 as follows:
xnorm,i =
xi–min(x)
max(x)–min(x)
(2.1)
where:
xnorm,i = The normalized value in the matrix
xi = The original value in the matrix
min(x) = The minimum value in the matrix
max(x) = The maximum value in the matrix
Data ordination
Procrustes analysis is used to compare ordination results. Therefore, ordination of the
distance data is needed as a preparatory step. Based on the nature of the data, the Prin-
cipal Coordinates Analysis (PCoA), or Classical Multidimensional Scaling was chosen as
the ordination method. The k-mer distance and the whole genome dissimilarity matrix
are both symmetric and contain non-Euclidean non-negative dissimilarity values. PCoA
is suitable to handle such data. It is used to reduce the multi-dimensional data to two
dimensions. The distance matrices are given as input to multidimensional scaling, which
12
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:18==
results in a set of points with distances between them roughly equal to the original dis-
tances in the matrix [50]. The ordination is performed using base R’s (v 4.3.1) cmdscale()
function.

Figure 2.3: Results of ordination of the gene cph2 (accessory gene encoding for putative
signaling protein) matrices: K-mer distance matrix (left) and whole genome distance
matrix of corresponding genomes (right)
Analysis
Symmetric Procrustes analysis was chosen to perform the distance matrix comparison. In
the case of symmetric Procrustes both the whole genome distance matrix and the flanking
region matrix are transformed to achieve the best possible alignment of the two matrices.
This includes transformations like scaling, translation and rotation (figure 2.4).

Figure 2.4: Asymmetric Procrustes analysis of the cph2 gene: A graphic representation of
how closely the two matrix ordinations match. Black points represent the initial ordina-
tion, blue arrows point to their positions in the target ordination.
The single gene’s degree of alignment then tells us whether its evolution has followed
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
13
==PAGE:19==
the same pattern or the same rate as entire genomes. The correlation between the two
matrices is used as a measure of similarity between whole genomes and flanking regions. A
high correlation suggests close alignment of the two matrices, suggesting similar evolution
patterns, while lower correlation values point to bigger differences between the two. The
Procrustes analysis is performed using vegan (v 2.6.4) R library [51] and its protest()
function. This function repeatedly performs the Procrustes symmetric analysis to estimate
the significance (p-value) of the correlation statistic. 999 permutations are used for the
significance calculation.
2.10.2
Mantel test
Whole Genome Matrix Reduction
Similar to Procrustes analysis, the whole genome similarity matrix had to be reduced. A
subset of the matrix was created to match the genomes contained in the ANI flanking
region matrix in order to perform the Mantel test.
Analysis
Mantel test was chosen to assess the relationship between the ANI of the flanking re-
gions and the whole genome similarity. Mantel test is a statistical method, that is used
to calculate correlation between two distance matrices. The calculated Mantel statistic
(correlation) was used as a measure of similarity of the two matrices. Significance of the
statistic is assessed through a series of permutations.
To perform the Mantel test, the function mantel() of the vegan (v 2.6.4) [51] R package was
used, thanks to its ability to handle missing values. In the rare cases, when FastANI [48] is
unable to calculate the ANI, NA’s are output in its place. Therefore, the option to remove
missing values from data was enabled. To calculate the test statistic 999 permutations
were used. The false discovery rate (FDR) adjusted p-values were calculated using the
p.adjust() R function.
2.11
Output: Gene metrics
The pipeline’s main output is a tab-separated file summarising the results of the analy-
ses. The results are merged using Python’s (v 3.7) pandas library. In addition to the
summary file, the full distance/similarity matrices for each target gene are provided as
supplementary output allowing for more detailed analysis.
gene_metrics.tsv contains the following columns:
• Gene: Gene cluster name as provided by Roary
• Kmer_distance_median: Per-gene median value of the flanking region k-mer dis-
tance matrix. It represents the distance of the flanking regions associated with each
gene cluster.
14
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:20==
• ANI_median: Per-gene median value of the flanking region average nucleotide iden-
tity matrix.
• Flank_index_median: Per-gene median value of the flanking region flank identity
index matrix. It represents the similarity of flank annotations associated with each
gene cluster.
• Proc_correlation: The correlation between the flanking region k-mer distances and
whole genome distances (Symmetric Procrustes analysis).
• Proc_significance: The significance value for Procrustes correlation.
• Man_correlation: The correlation between the flanking region ANI’s and whole
genome similarity (Mantel Test).
• Man_significance: The significance value of Mantel statistic (correlation).
2.12
Output post-processing
In this project, an analysis of the pipeline’s output to verify its functionality and demon-
strate its potential use was performed.
The data manipulation and visualisation were
performed using R (v 4.3.1), namely using the tidyverse (v 2.0.0) [52], ggplot2 (v 3.4.4)
[53] and ggrepel (v 0.9.4) [54] packages.
2.12.1
Visualisation of Roary results
The behaviour of the pangenome and how it varies with the addition of new genomes
(Figure 3.2) was visualised using Roary’s own R script create_pan_genome_plots.R, using
the output .Rtab files. Next, the core gene alignment from Roary was used to construct a
phylogeny tree of the analysed E.coli and Shigella genomes. For this purpose, FastTree [55]
was used and parameters -gtr -nt were specified to infer a tree for a nucleotide alignment
with the GTR+CAT model. The tree was visualised using iTOL [56]. The annotation
files were generated using table2itol R script [57].
2.12.2
Functional annotation
The target genes were functionally annotated. The Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and
Genomes (KEGG) [31] and Gene Ontology (GO) [32] are two of the most popular tools
for functional annotation. KEGG is a bioinformatics source containing databases that
link genes with their higher order functional information.
The GO is a set of terms
used to describe the functions of gene products (proteins) organised into three categories:
Biological Process (BP), Molecular Function (MF), and Cellular Component (CC). Both
KEGG’s PATHWAY database and GO were used to retrieve functional annotations of the
gene set.
The UniprotKB IDs were extracted for each of the target genes, filtering out the hypo-
thetical proteins that cannot be functionally annotated. Using Uniprot.ws (v 2.42.0) R
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
15
==PAGE:21==
package [58] the Uniprot IDs were converted to both the RefSeq identifiers and KEGG
IDs. The package uses the UniProt’s own ID translating tool to convert the IDs [59].
Next, the genes were filtered to keep only the ones curated by the RefSeq [36] and KEGG
Pathway databases [31], respectively. The RefSeq IDs were used for GO annotation, while
the KEGG IDs were used to extract pathway information from the KEGG database.
Both functional annotations were performed using clusterProfiler (v 4.10.0) R package
[60]. Function enrichKEGG() was used to retrieve the KEGG pathway information and
the enrichGO() function was used to annotate the genes based on the BP, MF and CC
GO subontologies.
2.13
Methods used in the initial pipeline version
2.13.1
Test dataset construction
The pipeline was designed and tested using a small dataset composed of 30 genomes: 19
E.coli genomes and 3 genomes of each Shigella species (S. dysenteriae,S. flexneri, S. boydii
and S. sonnei). All DNA sequences were downloaded manually from NCBI [35]. The E.coli
genomes were selected, so that each of the five most common E.coli phylogroups (A, B1,
B2, D, E) is represented within the data. The phylogroup of each genome was determined
using ClermonTyping [39]. Three genomes from each phylogroup were included in the
test dataset. Initially, to achieve a high level of diversity in the test data, each selected
E.coli genome, belonged to a different serotype. After that, in order to make the dataset
more representative of real-life data, more sequences for specific serotypes (O78:H4 and
O157:H7) that were already included in the dataset were added. With this addition, the
pipeline’s performance with more closely related genomes and more complex data could
be analysed.
2.13.2
Flankophile
When designing this work’s pipeline, the analysis and the visualisation of the flanking
regions of the test dataset was performed using Flankophile (v 0.2.9) [26].
The input
database for Flankophile was created using the Roary pan_genome_reference.fa file, con-
taining a cluster representative sequence for each core/accessory gene. The FASTA headers
were renamed to contain only the gene names and the sequences of target genes of interest
were extracted from the file using SeqKit (v. 2.1.0) [61]. Flankophile’s parameters were
then adjusted to be compatible with those of Roary. The minimum coverage compared to
reference sequence of ABRicate [27] was set to 98% and the minimum percentage identity
was lowered to 96%. The cluster identity and length of CD hit [28] was set to 0.95. K-mers
of size 16 bp and k-mer Hamming distance were used as distance measure. Flankophile
was used solely for the analysis of the test dataset due to the extensive computational
time and resources it required when processing large sized data.
16
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:22==
3
Results and Discussion
3.1
The pipeline design process
The first version of this work’s pipeline was designed in a way, that Flankophile, a gene
synteny analysis tool, would be used as the primary software for flanking region gene
synteny analysis [26]. Because Flankophile itself is a Snakemake pipeline, this workflow
was build to generate two types of output files that would complement Flankophile: 1. A
target-gene database, containing representative sequences of each gene cluster of interest
with unique headers and 2. an input list of sample names, file paths and their metadata.
The files could then be used directly as input files for Flankophile, creating the first
building block for a workflow that could be used to achieve a comprehensive analysis of
gene flux and gene synteny in bacteria. A user would be able to generate all the input
files necessary for Flankophile by executing a single command, seamlessly integrating the
data from the pangenome analysis and using them for the analysis and visualisation of the
flanking regions. Then, the results of Flankophile would be used in the third part of the
workflow, where additional metrics (flank index, ANI and whole genome distances) would
be calculated and the analysis of gene flux would be performed. The whole analysis would
be performed using 3 steps all created in Snakemake (Figure 3.1).

Figure 3.1: First Pipeline Design
When designing the pipeline, the test dataset (described in section 2.13.1) was used and
the execution of each command was evaluated in terms of computation time and memory
use. The first step was annotating the data using Prokka. The annotation of 30 genomes
required approximately 7 minutes of computing time on 40 CPU cores. The pangenome
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
17
==PAGE:23==
analysis was then performed using Roary. Initially, the execution time of Roary was much
longer than what was needed in order to scale-up with large data, exceeding 3 hours when
analysing 30 samples. An optimisation was performed, using faster MAFFT alignment for
aligning the core genes achieving a much more effective 23 minute-long run time. A total
of 12272 gene clusters were identified within the toy data. The core genome was composed
of 2569 genes, that were present in more than 99% of genomes, which coincided with the
literature research [4, 5, 6]. A total of 6715 accessory genes was identified (table 3.1).
Table 3.1: Summary statistics of Roary results using test-data

12272
A set of 76 known annotated genes was selected from Roary’s output and the reference
database was created. The hypothetical proteins were filtered out in order to obtain easily
interpretable data with well-known genes. All these steps were generalised in a Snakemake
pipeline creating part 1 of the workflow. Next, synteny analysis with Flankophile was
tested. First step of Flankophile is to align and locate the genes within the genomes of
interest using ABRicate [27]. Here I discovered, that ABRicate’s parameters had to be
adjusted, to yield results comparable to Roary’s. Flankophile’s default parameters (98%
coverage and 98% identity) were too strict. As a result, a core gene that was identified in
all 30 genomes in Roary, was only identified in 25 genomes using Flankophile. Minimum
identity had to be lowered to 96% to obtain comparable results. Flankophile also performs
its own clustering of gene hits. Therefore, the cluster identity of CD-HIT had to also be
lowered to 0.95, to match the parameters used by CD-HIT in Roary’s code. Having to make
these changes already hinted at the repetitive nature of the workflow being developed.
Despite these challenges, the execution of Flankophile on the test-data, has proven to
be both time and memory eﬀicient and seemed promising for a large-scale application.
Furthermore, the pipeline was eﬀicient despite of the choice to include much longer flanking
regions, than is Flankophile’s default (10 000 bp compared to default 1500 bp). The length
of 1500 bp would be too short to capture enough genetic context. This could include
presence of genome regions that could be longer than 1500 bp, such as transposon regions,
that play a crucial role in horizontal gene transfer.
Flankophile’s primary output are KMA’s distance matrices, showing the pairwise k-mer
distances between two genomes and plot visualisations showing a phylogeny tree and the
gene synteny. Its performance was first demonstrated on a set of antimicrobial resistance
genes [26]. Since ARGs occur in various genetic contexts and only occur in smaller number
18
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:24==
of genomes, they are the perfect use-case for the Flankophile pipeline.
However, our
reference database contained not only accessory genes, but also the ones belonging to
the core genome. This meant, that each core gene was present in each of the analysed
genomes, resulting in large and complex plots, even when analysing the small test data
(Figure 1). The output plots have proven diﬀicult to read and to identify similarities and
differences in the flanking region compositions. The extended flanking regions contained a
large number of genes, that were differentiated by colors, but the similarity in these colors
made it diﬀicult to effectively differentiate between them. In an attempt to then use the
k-mer distance matrices as a measure to compare the flanks, I discovered its limitations.
There were flanking regions, which had identical gene syntenies - the gene annotations
of the flanks and synteny plots were the same, but still exhibited high k-mer distance.
K-mer distance captures also single nucleotide mutations, that increase the number of
differing k-mers, but do not have any effect on the overall flank synteny and the gene
annotation stayed the same. Therefore a new measure, the flank identity index (section
2.9.2), was introduced as a replacement of the visual plots, allowing for easier comparison
when analysing large amount of samples. The calculation of the flank index was added as
a rule to the gene flux analysis part of the workflow (part 3).
After the first two building blocks of the workflow were designed and tested (Figure 3.1),
the real dataset, described in Section 2.2 was processed and the input files tailored for
Flankophile were generated. The execution of the first part of the pipeline, composed of
annotation and pangenome analysis, was successful and required 121 hours to run using
40 CPU cores on Computerome2 (The Danish National Supercomputer for Life Sciences)
thin node (192 GB memory available).
During the operation of Flankophile, many challenges emerged due to the large size of the
dataset. These were mainly associated with extended computational time requirements
for certain Snakemake rules responsible for data manipulation and parsing of files. During
this phase, I performed extensive optimization of individual rules to handle large-sized
data. The data manipulation scripts/rules within the pipeline were optimised to achieve
eﬀicient run times. In order to fit this project’s specific use-case, significant changes into
the original Flankophile code were made. At this point a decision was made to keep a
cloned version of the Flankophile repository in a separate location. This ensured that the
code would retain the changes regardless of the future updates to the oﬀicial repository.
However, despite the large modifications and considerable time spent on redesigning the
code, I have reached a bottleneck in the part of Flankophile, where annotation of the
flanking regions takes place. This was a direct consequence of the 10 000 bp flank length
and a large number of target genes. Selecting longer flanking regions led to their overlaps,
resulting in repetitive annotations of each target gene across the 3503 genomes in the
study. It has extended the estimated computation time of Flankophile to an extent, that
the annotation would not be able to finish within the projects time frame. Other options
to solve this problem were explored, but given the time-consuming nature of Flankophile,
the redundancy of some its steps, and the need for further optimization, I came to a
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
19
==PAGE:25==
conclusion that Flankophile was not the best solution for this project. Therefore, I re-
implemented Flankophile’s functionalities in a new pipeline, that is capable to analyse both
gene synteny and gene flux, on large-sized data. These functionalities were integrated in
the initial Snakemake file containing annotation and pangenome analysis.
Flankophile relies on ABRicate to gather information on the presence of genes in genomes.
The hits and their upstream and downstream flanking regions are then extracted from the
whole genome sequence, annotated and analysed. In contrast, I utilised the information
about the presence and location of each gene within the genomes from Roary and the
whole genome Prokka annotations for this purpose. These served as the base for creating
a new workflow described in Materials in Methods. The functionalities, such as whole
genome comparison using dRep, ANI and flank index calculation, the Procrustes analysis
and reporting of results that were originally meant to take place in a third separate script,
were added directly to the general pipeline. The main advantage of this approach was
that now a comprehensive analysis of gene synteny and flux could be executed by a single
command.
The use of well-curated reference genomes allowed to use the genome sequences directly as
the pipeline’s input without performing quality checks on the data. The pipeline, however,
is capable of taking all FASTA files as its input. This can include incomplete genomes
comprised of large contigs and genome assemblies. An essential step in the pipeline is
performed by Roary, which is sensitive to contamination.
In case a contamination is
present, Roary will not be able to identify any core genes within the data. For this reason
a quality check of input genomes using tools like Kraken [62] is recommended before
running the pipeline to obtain meaningful results.
The pipeline executed successfully on the large E.coli/Shigella whole genome data, pro-
viding an example of its functionality. The results, pipeline’s capabilities, limitations and
needed improvements are discussed in the following sections.
Summary of the results
discussed is shown in table 1.
3.2
E.coli and Shigella spp. pangenome
The base for the pipeline’s flux analysis is the calculation of the pangenome. The pangenome
composition of both Shigella and E.coli genomes was analysed. There were 68502 gene
clusters present in total. Based on Roary’s documentation, having more than 50000 gene
clusters generally means that a contamination in a form of unrelated species could be
present in the dataset. However, an exception can be made due to the large variability
in E.coli genomes, that are known to retain a large accessory genome, and the fact that
Shigella genomes were also included in the analysis. Additionally, there is a stable core
genome that is present amongst all the samples, which further confirms that no contami-
nation was present in the data.
The core genome, present in more than 99% of genomes, comprised of 2622 core gene
20
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:26==
clusters. These were in majority genes that encoded the essential cellular and metabolic
functions and were crucial for the bacteria’s survival.
The accessory genes were then
categorised into shell and cloud genes. The vast majority of genes belonged to the cloud
accessory genome (63498 genes were present in less than 15% of organisms), highlighting
the genetic diversity and large potential for horizontal gene transfer within E.coli and
Shigella spp.
Table 3.2: Summary statistics of Roary results using real-data

Bacterial pangenomes, including E.coli have been described as open, which means that
the number of gene clusters will continuously increase with the addition of new genomes
to the analysis [63]. This diversity of E.coli and Shigella species is apparent when looking
at how the pangenome changes when new genomes are added (Figure 3.2). Even with a
large dataset of genomes, addition of a new genome causes an increase in the total number
of genes and the pangenome is not saturating. However, there is a decrease in the number
of genes that get added with each genome over time. This means that even though the
gene gain continues, the rate of discovering entirely new genes gradually slows down. A
stable core genome of 2622 gene clusters is formed when a very few genomes are analysed
and remains unchanged with the addition of new genomes.

In recent analysis by Tantoso et. al (2022) [7], the pangenome of 1324 E.coli genomes was
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
21
==PAGE:27==
constructed. Similarly to these results, they have found a stable soft core genome (genes
present in >95% of genomes) composed of approximately 3000 genes. However, based on
their estimation, the total size of the E.coli pangenome should be approximately 25 000
gene families (clusters), which is significantly different compared to almost 70000 genes
that I have identified. It is expected, that with the increase in sample size (3503 genomes
in this project), more accessory genes will be discovered. This should be further magnified
by the inclusion of Shigella genomes in the data, which will in turn also contribute to
larger diversity. However, based on this project’s results in order to truly capture the
full diversity, especially the one of the accessory genome, even larger dataset would be
needed. It is possible, that these differences can be attributed to the identity threshold
I have set when performing the analysis using Roary. The minimum percentage identity
for pre-clustering with CD-HIT is 98% by default, while the identity for BLASTp to place
genes together in the same cluster was set to 95%. In contrast, Tantoso et. al (2022) have
applied much more relaxed criteria, with sequence length coverage and sequence identity of
CD-HIT both set to 60%. Having clustering parameters that are too strict, could therefore
lead to overestimation of the total number of gene clusters by essentially breaking up true
homologous clusters [63]. Based on the supplementary material, when applying stricter
parameters (80% sequence identity and 90% sequence length coverage), they identified over
43 000 gene families in the same data set [7]. It has been described, that when analysing
only 30 E.coli genomes, the change of the sequence identity and length coverage from 50
to 90% has lead to the increase in pangenome size from 13 000 to 18 000 gene clusters
[63]. Therefore, applying more relaxed criteria for Roary’s analysis should be explored
in the future. Additionally, pangenome calculation is largely influenced by annotation
errors. False positives are often predicted as CDS encoding hypothetical proteins, which
results in the increase of the number of gene clusters. In this analysis, tens of thousands
of hypothetical proteins (more than 70% of all genes) have been identified, which means
that the data possibly contained many mispredicted or uncharacterised sequences. Some
studies apply filtering steps before calculating the pangenome in order to remove these
from the data [63]. Filtering of hypothetical proteins from the data either before or after
performing the pangenome analysis should also be explored in further iterations of the
pipeline.
The MAFFT alignment of core genes was used to infer phylogeny using FastTree [55].
The phylogeny tree shows a clear clustering of Shigella and E. coli species. In addition, it
shows a separation of the phylogroups detected by the Clermont method [8]. The largest
portion of the strains belonged to phylogroup A (33%), followed by 19% and 18% of strains
belonging to B1 and B2, respectively. Phylogroups D and E were represented equally by
9% of strains.
The remainder of genomes belonged to the less common groups F, G,
cladeI and cladeV. The distribution of the main phylogroups suggests the dataset was well
diversed and chosen appropriately. The phylogeny correlates with findings from literature
where Shigella spp. is positioned as closest related to E.coli in phylogroups A1 and B1
[64, 65, 66].

3.3
Whole genome comparison
Using dRep, a comparison of all 3503 whole genome sequences was performed. The total
execution time of dRep was 5932 minutes (4,12 days) using 40 CPU cores on Comput-
erome2. Two pair-wise matrices were produced as a result: the similarity and the distance
matrix of the whole genome sequences. The similarities (or ANIs) between the genomes
ranged between 0.9297 and 1 (1 corresponding to 100% ANI), with a median value of
0.9758 (Figure 3.4). Despite the seemingly low minimum value, the vast majority of com-
parisons fell into range 0.96-100. As described by Jain et. al (2012) [48] organisms of
the same species show ANI values above 95%, underlining the shared origin and close
genetic relationship of E.coli and Shigella strains. The genome distances (dissimilarities),
as reported by dRep, were equal to 1 −similarity and therefore followed a reversed dis-
tribution. The genome distances, or dissimilarity values, ranged between 0 to 0.07 with a
median of 0.0242.
Even after excluding the diagonal from the pair-wise similarity matrix, there were simi-
larity values equal to 1 present in the data, indicating genomes that were 100% identical.
In total 63 FASTA files (genomes) showed 100% identity and all of these belonged to the
E.coli species. Since the ANI is only calculated using alignable regions, it does not nec-
essarily mean that these genomes are from the same strain. Nevertheless, these genomes
will behave as identical strains in further analyses. Since the main aim of the pipeline
is to analyse the (dis)similarity of the genomes and their evolutionary behavior, identical
samples can skew the results. In the future versions of the pipeline, new rules to filter
out sequentially redundant genomes (identity of 100%) need to be implemented before
proceeding with the comparisons.

3.4
Flanking region comparison
The 68502 gene clusters identified by Roary were filtered and a selection of 6343 target
genes was made. The gene set analysed composed of 947 core genes (present in 3327-3503
genomes), 596 shell genes (present in 527-3327 genomes) and 4800 accessory genes (present
in 10-527 genomes).
Three parameters that allow user to compare the identity of the flanking regions of target
genes in different genomes were calculated: average nucleotide identity, k-mer distance and
flank identity index. These three metrics together provide a comprehensive view on the
gene synteny identity. The k-mer distance matrices are useful when assessing the overall
differences between the individual flanks. They are a measure that best captures small,
single nucleotide differences between the flanking regions. In contrast the ANI can serve as
a complementary metric, especially for the genes, where the k-mer distances are low. For
very distant flanking regions, the ANI measure can be misleading, as it likely represents
only a few homologous regions. This is apparent from the paired scatterplot, where in
the region of very high ANI values 99-100% there are both genes with very high and very
low k-mer distances (Figure 3.5). FI is used as a third measure, providing insights into
the annotations of the genes contained within the flanking regions.
Sometimes, single
nucleotide mutations that occur during evolution can cause flanking regions to exhibit
high k-mer distances even though these mutations do not introduce any changes to the
protein that is being synthesized by the bacteria. Therefore, this index provides insight on
the functional conservation of genes around the target, thus providing a more biologically
relevant measure of similarity. This is helpful especially in cases where measures that focus
on nucleotide-level changes, like k-mer distance and ANI, are hard to interpret. FI seems
to be lower for accessory genes, and increases as it gets closer to the core genome.
24
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:30==
To provide a clear high-level overview of the gene mobility, the pipeline calculates the
median of each matrix (k-mer, ANI and flank index). The medians are a quick way to
compare a large number of genes and their synteny, and make it easy to see the patterns
and trends in the data (Figure 3.5). This approach can help the user identify potential
genes of interest for further investigation.
For this purpose, the pipeline also outputs
the complete k-mer distance, ANI and FI matrices, that can be used to conduct a more
thorough analysis.

The reasoning behind the selection of all three metrics is illustrated on the core gene aas
(Bifunctional protein Aas, UniprotKB: P31119), which was present in 3498 genomes. The
values in the gene’s individual matrices highlight the characteristics of each individual
metric (Figure 3.6).
In each of the pairwise flank comparisons, the ANI reaches high
values following a normal distribution centered around median of 98.60204. In contrast,
the k-mer distance matrix shows a bimodal distribution with two separate groups of values
within the data. Despite having great ANI, the majority of pairwise comparisons shows
k-mer distance of up to 40 000. Additionally, the k-mer matrix illustrates, that the choice
of a median, even though more robust than the mean, might not always provide the best
fit for the data (Figure 3.6).
The flank identity index provides more tangible results.
It shows that the functional identity of the flanking regions is more than 80% in most
comparisons, despite the high k-mer distances.

However, the version of the flank index currently implemented in this pipeline is fairly
simple and in this form has its limitations. The metric works well when the flanks are
composed of mostly well-annotated genes, as was the case of the aas gene shown above.
The biggest problems are caused by the presence of hypothetical proteins in the flanking
regions. These proteins do not have unique gene or UniProt IDs, making them impossible
to distinguish. In practice this means, that if a flanking region of two genes being compared
are composed of 10 hypothetical proteins in both cases, current version of the pipeline is
unable to diferrentiate whether these are the same genes or if some of them differ. In
these instances, the flank index is impossible to calculate and only outputs ”NA” as a
result.
This issue is also present if only a small number of genes are annotated and
comparable, because a large portion of the flanking regions gets omitted, which could
introduce uncertainty to the results. This problem is similar to the one of Flankohile’s plots
(Figure 1), where hypothetical proteins are all represented by a grey arrow and can’t be
differentiated with certainty. One possible solution is matching the cluster names assigned
to each hypothetical protein by Roary back to its corresponding Prokka annotations. This
way, it would be possible to differentiate the hypothetical proteins, making the flank index
an even more powerful tool.
3.5
Whole genome and flanking region distance
comparison
The initial plan was to use the flanking region ANI matrices (section 2.9.3) to perform the
comparison to whole genomes using Procrustes analysis. However, there was a limitation
in this approach. FastANI introduces a cut-off value for largely dissimilar DNA sequence
regions of 80%.
When the ANI is below this threshold, the output of FastANI is an
26
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:32==
”NA” value. When testing the pipeline on smaller data, the flanking regions have shown
high identity, leading to the assumption that this would be true for large data as well.
Therefore, the presence of NAs in the data was not anticipated. It’s important to note that
Procrustes analysis is not able to handle NAs. This means that, if a single cell in a row or
column contained a missing value, the whole row and column would need to be removed,
as removing only a single cell would disrupt the matrix’s structure and dimensions. This
removal would result in excluding entire genomes from the data, most likely the ones
containing flanking regions that differed the most. Since using FastANI matrices would
provide unreliable results and significantly reduce the dataset, a decision was made to use
the k-mer distance matrices instead.
In the first attempt, the comparison was performed using asymmetric Procrustes analysis.
The whole genome distance matrix serves as a reference and remains unchanged, while the
flanking region matrix is transformed to fit this reference to achieve maximum similarity
and minimize the sum of squares (SS). The lower the SS, the closer is the fit of the matrices.
The whole genome distance matrix was reduced to mirror the samples in the k-mer matrix.
This meant, that in case of the core genome, the matrices were of size 3503x3503, and
in case of the rarest accessory genomes, they were as small as 10x10 observations in size.
The analysis of the sums of squares of each fit has shown that the value of the sum is
largely influenced by the size of the matrix - the gene occurrence. The SS and Occurrence
seemed to have a linear relationship (Figure 3.7).
0.0
0.2
0.4
0
1000
2000
3000
Occurence
Procrustes SS
Figure 3.7: Relationship between Procrustes sum of squares and gene occurrence (Asym-
metric Procrustes analysis)
This was further confirmed by linear regression, where the R-squared value of 0.9628
indicated that a large portion of the variance in the sum of squares is explained by the
number of genomes the gene was present in, or in other words, the size of the matrix.
The p-value was low (< 2.2e-16) confirming the linear relationship. Because of this, it
was necessary to normalise the sum of squares measure by dividing it by the occurrence
in order to obtain comparable data for both core and rare genes (Figure 3.8).
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli

The vast majority of the core genes have exhibited similar values of normalised SS with a
median of 0.000158 (Figure 3.8). These genes are essential for the overall functioning and
survival of the organism. The narrow range of SS values of core genes suggests that they
have evolved at a similar manner across all analysed samples and their evolution, aligned
with the overall evolution of the bacterial species, is consistent. This uniformity among the
core genes was expected, considering the core genes are placed stable within the genome
and have evolved over extended periods across a large number of genomes. However, it
was also expected that these values would serve as a baseline for the closest alignment to
the whole genome evolution. Surprisingly, the accessory (shell and cloud) genes show a
much wider spread, with values ranging from 2.67e-08 to 0.00029 and a median of 0.0001
(Figure 3.8). This would mean that a large portion of the accessory genome shows a much
closer alignment to the whole genome distances, than the majority of core genes and that
their evolution is identical to the one of the corresponding bacterial strains.
This raised a question, whether these values truly reflect a very close alignment with
the whole genome phylogeny, or whether these findings were a result of an uncertainty
introduced by the calculations. Despite the normalisation, the fact that the accessory
gene matrices were smaller should be taken into account, as this may increase the level of
computation uncertainty. While these results are notable, the asymmetric Procrustes does
not provide a significance value that could be used to verify them. This is why a decision
was made to employ the symmetric Procrustes analysis (described in section 2.10.1), and
use the correlation as the means to assess how well the flank and whole genome distances
align. The symmetric method also provides statistical significance for the results.

Consistent with the previous findings, the core genes showed similar behaviour (Figure
3.9).
Their range of correlation values was much narrower than the one of accessory
genome, ranging from 0.053 to 0.37 with a median value of 0.177. However, the degree of
correlation between the core gene distances and the whole genome distances was surpris-
ingly low considering that a greater degree of alignment between overall genomic distances
and core gene distances was anticipated. These results were all confirmed by a low p-value
of 0.001 for all core genes analyzed. In contrast, accessory genes with a high level of cor-
relation were identified despite the previous assumption that these were merely a result
of computation uncertainty. The accessory and shell genome indeed exhibited variable
p-values, confirming that, in some cases, a small number of observations can lead to un-
reliable results. Nevertheless, accessory genes with a correlation close to 1.0, that show
almost identical distances to those of the whole genomes and are also supported by low
test statistics are present. As an example a hypothetical protein (ID group_49992), which
showed high correlation of 0.995, which was also confirmed by a significance value of 0.001
is shown in Figure 3.10.

Figure 3.10: Relationship between k-mer distance matrix of group_49992 gene cluster and
whole genome distance.
Genes with even stronger correlation were identified, however their significance values
exceeded the conventional threshold of 0.05. This might be due to the presence of identical,
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
29
==PAGE:35==
or very similar E.coli/Shigella strains in the data (Figure 3.4). Having identical genomes
in the dataset could have caused, that a distance matrix of a gene that was also in these
identical strains, would be compared to a whole genome dissimilarity matrix filled with 0
values. Same would be true for the flanking region distance matrix, since these regions
originated from the identical genomes as well. Matrices that consist mostly of zeroes have
the potential to artificially increase the correlation coeﬀicient. Correlation is a measure of
the degree of linear relationship between variables, and in a dataset where there is little to
no variation, this relationship will be strong. In such case, the p-values reflect this issue.
There could be multiple reasons, why the results shown that the distances of core genes
do not correlate with those of the whole genome. Biologically, during evolution, the core
genes are under strong purifying selection. During this process the harmful genetic variants
are eliminated from the population [67]. The whole genome, however, contains sequence
regions under different selective pressures, that can mutate and evolve much faster. This
means that the evolution of core genome sequence regions can be much slower than the
overall evolution of the whole genome. Their distances will not align perfectly with the core
gene distances, which could explain the low correlation of the core gene distance matrices
to whole genome dissimilarities.
Furthermore when dealing with large-sized matrices,
like those that represent genomes the chances of observing strong correlations naturally
decrease. This is because larger datasets tend to contain a diversity of variations and
evolutionary events, resulting in more noise and fluctuations in the data. As a result,
relationships might be masked by this complexity, especially when comparing genomic
data with more specialized gene sets.
However, the most probable reason is the behaviour of the k-mer and average nucleotide
distance matrices, which was illustrated in Figure 3.6. Since k-mer distance matrices show
completely different distributions than the ones produced by dRep, they might simply not
be compatible with each other for direct comparison in Procrustes analysis. It could also
explain why high correlations are only observed in small matrices, where these distributions
are more likely to align by chance.
The k-mer distance and ANI matrices were also
compared using an alternative Mantel test, but this test produced the same results.
To verify that the matrices were indeed incompatible, the ANI flank matrices and the
whole genome similarity matrices were compared as originally intended. For this purpose,
the Mantel test was selected, because of its ability to handle missing values. Therefore, if
a single NA is present within a row of a matrix, there is no need to remove the whole row
to retain the dimensions of the matrix as it was in the case of Procrustes analysis. The
correlation of the target gene ANI and the whole genome similarity is shown in Figure
3.11. The majority of the comparisons show low significance values (below 0.05). Since
the null hypothesis for Mantel test is that there is no correlation between the matrices,
values over 0.05 only occur in the group of genes, where the correlation values are close to
0 or negative. The FDR (false discovery rate) corrected p-values were also calculated and
are shown in Figure 3 in the Appendix.

Figure 3.11: Mantel Test - Correlation of target gene’s flank ANI and whole genome
similarity: A - Scatter plot colored by significance values, B - Blue boxplot = Core and

All the genes seemingly showed the same range of correlation values across the pangenome.
However, due to overplotting of values on the scatterplot, boxplots were generated to anal-
yse the distribution of the correlations of core and accessory genes more precisely. These
results align closely with our initial expectations, where the core genome shows a statis-
tically significant high level of correlation to the whole genome sequences with a narrow
spread of values. As the occurrence values decrease and get closer to the accessory genome,
the range gets wider and the correlation decreases for the values in the interquartile range.
Interestingly, the core genome is the only one showing the presence of outliers in the data,
suggesting there are genes among the core genome that show higher mobility that is the
average of this pangenome group and could be subject to investigation.
Currently, both the symmetric Procrustes analysis and Mantel test are integrated as part
of the pipeline. Based on the results, it is apparent that having the same distance measures
in both matrices is crucial to obtain reliable results. Therefore, rules for k-mer distance
calculation for the whole genome sequences will be added to the pipeline and used in
Procrustes analysis instead.
3.6
Functional annotation
In order to make the pipeline robust to all types of data, the hypothetical proteins were
also included in the analysis alongside well-known genes, when the final set of genes was
selected. However, as the selection was done by random chance, out of the 6700 genes
analysed, around 4900 were hypothetical proteins not present in the UniProt database.
These might provide valuable insights in the future, however for the purpose of this project,
only the 1849 genes contained within the UniProt database were analysed in terms of
mobility and its relationship to gene functionality.
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
31
==PAGE:37==
The random selection unfortunately created a problem I did not anticipate. The genes were
sampled randomly from highest to the lowest occurrence, taking every 3rd gene. However,
closer to the edge of the accessory genome, and with decreasing gene occurrence, the
number of detected hypothetical proteins increases. This is, of course, also due to the
fact, that the core genome is better researched and therefore the function of the genes
in the core genome is well-known. In the accessory genome, there is a larger portion of
genes with unknown function. This meant that there was a higher probability, that the
selected genes will fall into the hypothetical proteins category. When performing functional
annotation, the hypothetical proteins had to be filtered out, skewing the composition of
the data significantly towards the core genome, when compared to the original distribution
of the data (Figure 3.12).
0

2000
3000
0
1000
2000
3000
