Frequency
Group
All Genes
Hypothetical Proteins
Figure 3.12: The hypothetical proteins filtered out before functional enrichment: Blue -
original distribution of all genes, red - hypothetical proteins.
The remaining genes were used to retrieve information on function from KEGG and GO
terms. First, the UniProtKB IDs were converted to KEGG IDs. Out of the 1849 UniProt
IDs, 158 were not curated by the KEGG Database and failed to map (Figure 3.13). The
mapped IDs were used to retrieve the Pathway information from the KEGG database.
Unfortunately, only 559 pathways were able to match to the KEGG IDs provided. The
majority of the data did not get annotated, which suggests there are potential limitations to
the approach. These counts were confirmed when manually searching the KEGG database
using the KEGG ID list generated by Uniprot.ws conversion. As the KEGG database is
one of the largest sources for functional gene enrichment and annotation, it is likely that
the issue lies in the conversion of the UniProt IDs to the KEGG IDs.
The GO annotation was performed using the converted RefSeq IDs of the 1849 target
genes. The mapping of UniProt IDs to RefSeq IDs was more successful, with only 83 genes
failing to map to the RefSeq Database. The remainder of genes were annotated based on
their Biological Process, Molecular Function and Cellular Component GO subontologies.
A corresponding BP annotation was assigned to 1061 genes, MF to 959 genes and CC to
375 unique genes (Figure 3.13).

The largest identified KEGG group of genes in the data was involved in the biosynthesis
of secondary metabolites, which are often specific to only a group of strains (Figure 3.16).
Two−component signal transduction systems, that enable bacteria to adapt to intra- and
extracellular changes were the second most frequent group [68]. ABC (ATP-binding cas-
sette) transporters presented the third most abundant group. They are one of the largest
protein groups in KEGG. Through hydrolysis of ATP, they facilitate active transport of
several substrates, including ions, sugars, lipids, sterols, peptides and proteins [68]. Pro-
teins involved in Microbial metabolism in diverse environments and Fructose and mannose

As shown by the success rate of the KEGG and GO annotation (Figure 3.13), only a small
sample of genes was present and analysed within each pathway and it would be therefore
diﬀicult to draw any concrete conclusions. Adding the option to filter out hypothetical
proteins when constructing the gene list, or allowing the user to select their own set of
genes to analyse, will be a priority in future iterations of the pipeline. Changes will also
have to be made to the way the data from the KEGG database are retrieved, as currently
it is not effective.
The UniProt.ws library is used to translate UniProt identifiers to
KEGG identifiers. When searching the UniProt database, in some cases it happened that
these were matched to genes from organisms other than E.coli, for example Salmonella
or Streptomyces.
UniProtIDs are assigned based on protein sequences, so if a protein
sequence from E. coli is highly similar to a protein in another organism, they may have
wrong UniProtID assigned.
As a result, these got matched to the wrong KEGG IDs
and subsequently to a wrong pathway. Only pathways retrieved from Shigella and E.coli
KEGG IDs were kept in the data, which resulted in further loss of information. Due to
the the reduced size of the dataset, the analysis of the gene function and their relation to
the whole genome behaviour was conducted only to demonstrate the potential use cases
of this pipeline.
3.7
Pipeline use case: functional analysis
Thanks to KEGG enrichment, it is possible to analyze gene flux based on the pathways in
which the genes function. Three groups (pathways) of genes and their flanking regions were
selected to analyse their behavior: genes involved in ribosome formation, genes involved
in beta-lactam resistance and genes involved in Shigellosis. Ribosomes are essential in
protein synthesis, therefore this group was selected to represent chromosomal genes, that
are naturally expected in the core genome. In contrast, the genes from the beta-lactam
34
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:40==
resistance pathway were selected, because of its potential to contain accessory genes known
to exhibit high gene mobility, potentially located on plasmids.
The genes involved in
Shigellosis were chosen as a combination of the two. They are located on large virulence
plasmids specific to Shigella spp. and a sub-group of enteroinvasive E. coli. These were
selected as representatives of plasmid genes, that are likely to occur in a large number of
Shigella spp. contributing to their core genome.

Figure 3.16: The relationship of Mantel correlation and occurence based on the KEGG

In the analysed dataset, which included a total of 198 Shigella genomes, the genes asso-
ciated with Shigellosis are technically accessory genes, however they have shown charac-
teristics similar to those of the core genome (Figure 3.11). Genes that were categorised in
this group include virA, virB, virF, ospF, ospG, mxiH, and iscA. All of these were previ-
ously identified in the sequences of virulence plasmids, such as pCP301 [69], pWR100 [70]
and pINV_F6_M1382 [71], all described in Shigella flexneri. Their behavior, in terms of
the Mantel correlation, closely mirrors the behaviour of the core genome. They showed
notably high correlation with the whole genome matrix, ranging from 0.59 to 0.84 (Figure
3.16). Given these results, it’s highly likely that in the sub-set of Shigella genomes, the
genes involved in Shigellosis are core due to their essential role in the pathogenicity of
these bacteria. Additionally, it seems that the baseline of Mantel correlation range that
was established by the core genome (Figure 3.11) can truly serve as a reference for gene
conservation and mobility.
In contrast, all genes involved in ribosome formation were placed in the group of core genes.
Surprisingly, flanking regions of numerous ribosomal genes are exhibiting correlation below
0.5 to the whole genome similarity, and are positioned as outliers of the Mantel correlation
plot (Figure 3.16). It is known, that genes encoding ribosomal proteins are among the
most essential for the survival of bacteria and are a highly conserved gene group, with
lower evolutionary rates. Therefore it is very unlikely, that these genes are the ones that
tend to change location within the genome. However, in bacterial genomes these genes
are often found in clusters (operons) together with other genes essential for transcription
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
35
==PAGE:41==
and translation. This could mean that ribosomal genes that are in the center of these
clusters could overall exhibit much lower rate of change, mirroring the high conservation
of ribosomal genes. On the other hand the genes located on the edges of these clusters
could have gene neighbourhood that is less conserved. Mobilisation would be more likely
to occur in the genes surrounding them. Therefore, conducting experiments with shorter
flanking regions could reduce this effect and provide an explanation for the behaviour of
ribosomal genes.
Lastly, the beta-lactam resistance pathway was analysed. The resistance to beta-lactam
antibiotics in this pathway is achieved by many ways, which can include: beta-lactamase
production, altering the beta-lactam targets of penicillin-binding proteins, reduction of
transport of beta-lactams into the cell by changing the porins and others [68]. Genes act-
ing in this pathway were separated into two groups based on their Mantel correlation and
occurrence. The first group consisted of core genes (acrA, acrB_1 and mppA) with cor-
relation above 0.7. The beta-lactam resistance pathway comprises of a broad set of genes.
The acrA and acrB genes, for example, are part of a multidrug efflux system, which not
only takes part in beta-lactam resistance, but also in cationic antimicrobial peptide re-
sistance [68]. Therefore, the presence of these genes in all strains does not necessarily
mean, that the resistance to beta-lactam antibiotics is a universal characteristic of all the
analysed strains. These genes can be participating in other pathways essential to bacte-
rial survival. The second group, however, consisted of accessory genes with correlation
values below 0.35. Such low values of the Mantel statistic indicate a high degree of gene
mobility. Apart from gene ompc_2 (Outer membrane protein C, UniProtID: Q8CVW1),
all other genes were encoding the beta-lactamase enzyme. It is also notable, that gene
bla_2 exhibited no correlation to the whole genome phylogeny, despite being present in
664 genomes.
Interestingly, genes both involved in Shigellosis and beta-lactam resistance pathways are
located on plasmids, yet they show different behaviour. The Shigellosis pathway genes fall
within the baseline of high core gene correlation, while the beta-lactamase encoding genes
show very low values. This major difference can be attributed to several factors related to
the nature of the plasmids and the genes they carry. Shigella virulence genes are located on
large, stable plasmids, that are typically specific to different Shigella strains and maintain
a consistent genetic profile. The stability within specific Shigella strain will result in high
correlation value with the evolution of the whole strain.
Additionally, Shigellosis is a
disease specific to only Shigella spp. and therefore these genes/plasmids are less likely
to be transferred horizontally between unrelated bacterial species. On the other hand,
ARGs are well-known for their capability to take part in horizontal gene transfer. The
genes involved in beta-lactam resistance can be located in various genetic contexts and
on a large number of different plasmids that are easily transferred between species, thus
contributing to the spread of antimicrobial resistance. These results confirm, that genes
with flanking regions that are variable, tend to show lower correlation to the whole genome
distances and that they will fall bellow the correlation threshold set by the core genome.

The highly mobile plasmid beta-lactamase genes were selected and compared with chromo-
somal ribosome genes in terms of their flanking regions. The median k-mer distances and
flank indexes are shown on Figure 3.17. There is a clear separation between these groups.
The accessory resistance genes show high k-mer distances, ranging between 30-40000. In
addition, their flanking regions differ also in terms of synteny, which is represented by the
flank index. Between different genomes, their flanking regions only share between 12.5
- 41.3% of genes. On the other hand, the core ribosomal genes show fairly low k-mer
distances despite being compared among significantly larger number of genomes. Their
median identity of flanking genes is within a high range 75-100%. This means that indeed
the genes with low identity in their flanking regions will likely show lower correlation to
the whole genome evolution, while in contrast the genes that are highly conserved, will
tend to show similar behaviour.
The GO terms can be used to gain even more information about the data. The genes
involved in Ribosome pathway (synthesis of ribosomal RNA and Ribosomal proteins)
showed unusually high variance in correlation to the behaviour of the whole core gene
set (Figure 3.16).
Since the ribosomal genes were well annotated even in GO terms,
we believed these might help us understand what differentiates these genes. Using the
CC annotation, the ribosomal gene products were separated to those that make up small
ribosomal subunit, and to those that build up the large subunit. Unfortunately, no distinct
patterns were observed neither in terms of Mantel Correlation, nor the Flank index and
k-mer distance (Figure 3.19).

The analysis using molecular function GO subontology revealed that indeed the rRNA
binding proteins show a much higher variance, even when compared with mRNA and
especially tRNA binding proteins (Figure 3.18). The tRNA binding proteins showed a
very narrow range of high correlations with the whole genomes. All three groups composed
of exclusively core genes and they were approximately the same size (17 observations in
rRNA, 15 observations in tRNA group, and 14 observations in mRNA group). The unique
behavior of ribosomal genes becomes higly interesting for potential future analysis, since
genes encoding for tRNA and mRNA binding are also considered to be highly conserved,
but their correlation values do fall inside the core genome threshold.

Interestingly, the beta-lactamase genes were not matched with any GO terms, neither CC,
BP or MF, despite the GO annotation being more successful. The beta-lactam resistance
genes were unfortunately also the only group of ARGs present in the data, that was
represented by more than a single gene and therefore there is not enough data to compare
38
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:44==
them with. The fact that there was only a few ARGs, in the final gene list could be due to
several reasons. They might have been missed by the random selection, or these genes have
been present in less than 10 genomes and filtered out. Third option is that large number
of plasmid genes had to be filtered out due to insuﬀicient flank lengths. Plasmid sizes in
E.coli can range from 1 - 200< kb. Genes that were located on plasmids smaller than 20
kb, would be filtered out automatically, because the flanking regions themselves are 20 kb
large, without accounting for the target gene length. Additionally, if the AMR genes were
located at the beginning of the plasmid sequence, the pipeline would not be able to extract
the regions either. Since many genes involved in the Shigellosis pathway were successfully
identified, there is reason to believe that this was the issue. The virulence plasmids in
Shigella spp. are large in size, for example, the DNA sequence of the pCP301 plasmid is
composed of 221 kb and plasmid’s pWR100 sequence is 213 kb long [70, 69]. The large
size of the plasmid made it possible to extract numerous virulence genes, without facing
the issue of insuﬀicient flank length.
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
39
==PAGE:45==
4
Conclusion
This project initially aimed to be heavily focused on the analysis of the gene flux in E.
coli. However, since Flankophile could not be implemented within the pipeline, the focus
of this project has shifted from conducting of thorough analysis of genetic flux in E.coli
and Shigella spp. to designing a completely new pipeline capable to do so. This change in
direction has lead to developing of a stand-alone tool capable of comparing the flanking
regions of target genes, as well as comparing their distances/similarities with the ones of the
associated whole genomes. The pipeline calculates a selection of metrics - k-mer distance,
flank identity index and ANI - that best describe the differences between the flanking
sequences, and Procrustes analysis and Mantel test are used as the methods for comparing
them with the full genome sequences. In the end, this project resulted in a pipeline that
can take raw genome sequences as input, and provide a comprehensive overview of each
gene’s flanking regions and their relationship with whole genome phylogeny using a single
command.
During the conducted analysis of results, limitations of the pipeline were identified. These
will need to be addressed to remove uncertainties from the data. It is apparent, that
two aspects had the biggest negative impact on the final results. Firstly, the selection
of the gene set from the results of the pangenome analysis. Many ambiguous sequences
(hypothetical proteins) were introduced into the target gene dataset, which after filtering,
resulted in a significant reduction of the data size. Secondly, the process of comparing the
whole genome distances to the flank distances was initially not possible, due to the selection
of distance measures that were not compatible with each other, neither in Procrustes
analysis, nor in the Mantel Test.
Arguably, this method provides more information about the gene flux that could be ob-
tained if Flankophile was used as the primary tool.
The reliability of the results was
showcased by analysing genes with known behavior in terms of gene mobility.
It was
shown that the core genes are likely to be highly correlated with the whole genome phy-
logeny, and in turn could serve as a baseline to identify genes that are highly conserved.
This was demonstrated with the Shigella virulence genes. Despite being accessory genes,
they are crucial for the infectious capabilities of Shigella spp. and therefore behave like
core genes in this subset of genomes. Additionally, the results have shown, that genes that
are known to exhibit high mobility, such as ARGs, tend to show very low relationship to
whole genome phylogeny.
This leads us to believe, that while no new findings were obtained from the results at this
time, this pipeline is well suited for a large scale analysis of gene flux in virtually any
bacterial species and has the potential to discover new insights into gene behaviour.
40
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
==PAGE:46==
4.1
Outlook
This report describes the first working version of the gene flux analysis pipeline, therefore
there are several technical improvements that need to be made in the future. One of them
is making the pipeline more robust by implementing thorough error and temporary files
handling. Currently, the pipeline relies on the user to provide input files in the correct
format. In the future iterations, file checks will be implemented in the beginning of the
script, in order to prevent errors that could potentially cause the pipeline to break at
later stages of analysis. Currently, all intermediate files are retained after the analysis is
finished. They were used to test the performance of the pipeline as well as to validate the
correctness of these output files. However, many of these files are not necessary for the gene
flux analysis. These files occupy a significant amount of disk space and in the next versions
will be changed to temporary. These will be deleted by default once they are no longer
necessary for the analysis to continue. An option to keep these files will be implemented as
well. The output files will be limited to annotation files, results of pan-genome analysis,
individual gene distance matrices and the main summary file.
Additionally, reporting
capabilities will be introduced to the pipeline. Report files will be generated to provide
information on the results of gene filtering and exclusion of genes, when the length of
the flanking region is insuﬀicient, to help the user understand what happens in individual
stages of the pipeline.
Pipeline’s analytic functionalities will also have to be optimised further.
As discussed
in section 3.5, the Procrustes analysis needs to be conducted on same type of distance
matrices. Rules to index and calculate k-mer hamming distance matrices of the whole
genome sequences will be added and tested. These calculations were not included in this
version of the pipeline, due to the project’s limited time frame. The capability to analyse
the relationship of gene function and mobility will be added. However, it will not be an
integrated part of the pipeline. Instead a complementary R script constructed to perform
the functional annotation will be uploaded to the repository. The reason for this is that
the objectives and scope of the functional analysis might differ significantly based on the
user’s individual research objectives. This approach allows the user to tailor the analysis
to their specific needs.
After refining the technical aspects of the pipeline, further research will focus on refin-
ing the set of genes that is analysed, most importantly excluding hypothetical proteins
from the analysis and focusing on well-annotated genes.
This approach will allow for
interpretable results, because the functional characteristics of these genes are better un-
derstood. Additionally, expanding the dataset will allow us to investigate patterns of gene
flux related to abundance of the genes in the pan-genome, as well as to their biological
function. So far, the association of the flank distances with the whole genome distances
was explored. These results provide insight especially on whether genes evolved vertically,
or whether an instance of HGT was present during evolution. Experiments with shorter
flanking regions (e.g 5000 bp) should also be performed and compared with those using
Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
41
==PAGE:47==
longer flanking sequences. Close association between short flanks and target genes, but
lack of association to a longer flank could suggest moving within a genome. These ex-
periments, along with a larger gene dataset, could also provide answers to the observed
behaviour of outliers within the core genome correlation values.

Genome-wide analysis for the quantification of gene flux in E.coli
